Appendix E
REPORT OF THE
CHRISTIAN EDUCATION AND PUBLICATIONS COMMITTEE
TO THE EIGHTH GENERAL ASSEMBLY
OF THE PRESBYTERIAN CHURCH IN AMERICA
INTRODUCTION
A. Philosophy
“ For the perfecting (equipping) of the saints, unto the work of ministering, unto the building up of the
body of Christ . . . that we may be no longer children, tossed to and fro and carried about with every wind of
doctrine . . . but speaking truth in love, may grow un in all things into him, who is the head, even Christ; from
whom all the body fitly framed and knit together through that which every joint supplieth . . . "(Eph. 4:12-16).
The PCA’s permanent Committee for Christian Education and Publications is attempting to follow the
above instruction from the Scripture. It has kept as its purpose equipping the saints for ministry. The goals are
propagating sound doctrine, encouraging interpersonal relationships and communication within the Church
and beyond. We have attempted to combine the practice of truth and love, emphasizing the interdependence of
each member with the other.
Because Ephesians 4:14 emphasizes God's will that His Church be highly doctrinal, and because
Ephesians 4:15 underscores that truth must be combined with love, and because Ephesians 4:16 teaches that
believers are part of Christ’s body, hence in need of each other, Christian Education and Publications through
its programs has attempted to keep these basic foundational truths before the Church.
All of our materials and programs have been provided with the hopes and desires that the PCA as a
denomination and its particular churches will demonstrate the principles set forth in Ephesians 4:12-16.
B. Present Programs
we have worked from a firm conviction that without a solid foundation the church will not fulfill her
task. Hence, we have concentrated on building and strengthening of foundations. Growing out of this, we have
made available programs for local churches and presbyteries that, we believe, assist them in growing towards
the likeness of the Church that is described in Scripture.
Members of the Committee and staff have personally worked within churches and presbyteries to give
instruction first to the officers of the church. We have acted from a firm conviction that the church leaders
(officers) are the key to church growth. We have given special attention to the area of officer training both from
a seminar level of instruction and from developing and recommending materials that we believe to be crucial to
good Christian leadership.
Through our Christian Education seminars, we have helped numerous churches develop Christian
Education programs that are enabling leaders and teachers to have an impact on the life of the churches as the
Scripture is studied, taught and applied locally.
Because 1980 is the 200th anniversary of the modem Sunday School movement, we have given
particular attention to that aspect of church training and development within the total framework of the Church
School and her ministry. The modem Sunday School, statistically, continues to be the number one key in
church growth and discipleship. Our instruction and materials are designed self-consciously to stimulate
Christian growth, through evangelism and education.
We have given particular attention to family ministries on the basis that there has to be close interdepend
ence of the home and the church in equipping God’s covenant people. Through family and marriage seminars,
conferences, resources for Christian Day Schools and devotional materials, we are attempting to place strong
emphasis on the home, as God's basic unit within His creation. We have continually encouraged strong
coordination between the home and the church in the training of children, youth and adults.
C. Future Programs
We are anxious to continue to expand our programs and resources in the near future. The area of
audio-visuals continues to be a most needed area of concentration. The CE/P Committee is aware that without
the prayers and support of the denomination it cannot carry out its task. As you read the details of our work
contained in this report, we solicit your prayers and support. We must not delay nor fail to take advantage of
God’s opened door in the area of discipleship (Christian growth) through Christian Education.
I. CURRICULA
A. Adult Bible Series
The adult education series of Bible study, a six-and-one-half-year project, is now halfway completed.
Publication of the accompanying family devotional materials was dropped during the past year due to lack of
funds. However, the use of the Bible study materials has been most encouraging.
B. Adult Doctrinal Series
The Christian Education and Publications Committee presently is now preparing a six volume doctrinal
series, setting forth the distinctive systematic doctrines of our Presbyterian faith. The project will be a unique
approach to the study of theology using accompanying visual materials.
C. Diaconal Materials
A rough draft of the manual on “ Diaconal Ministries" is being edited, and the publication should be
available for church use this fall.
D. Other Materials
A Bible memory course is now available for children. "With My Whole Heart", a study for the WIC is
also available. There are now nine different titles for local WIC's. The new study of 1982 will be on prayer.

<a id="ga08-p171"></a>
<!-- PAGE ga=8 pdf_page=171 printed_page=null -->

A recommendation pertaining to the printing of the Presbyterian Church in America doctnnal standards
follows.
We would call attention also to other materials available from the Christian Education and Publications
office such as the PCA tracts, certificates and a variety of recommended materials. A full line of audio-visual
equipment is also available through the bookstore.
The CE/P staff continues to review materials from other publishers in order to assist local churches in the
area of resources. A quarterly bulletin with Christian Education news and ideas will be mailed to your local
church. This will be a further attempt to be of assistance to churches in keeping them aware of resources
available for their Christian Education programs.
E. Great Commission Publications (GCP)
GPC, a joint venture of the Orthodox Presbyterian Church and the Presbyterian Church in America has
developed a substantial body of material for local church education. Three major projects within the past
year have been the new senior high program, the final year of the Vacation Bible School materials, and several
adult study books.
Working jointly with GCP, CE/P is presently developing pre-school material. If the funding of this
project is realized, these materials will be offered for use in September, 1981. Mrs. Susan Hunt of the CE/P
staff and Mrs. Dorothy Anderson of the GCP staff are working together on this needed project.
The present program of GCP calls for the production of 16 adult studies and the revision of the Primary
and Junior High materials over the next three years.
A good number of PCA churches have begun using GCP titles during the past year. A total of 58% of PCA
congregations are now using this Assembly-approved material.
There are more than 350 churches outside the two parent denominations using the GCP materials. This
gives us an important outreach ministry and the hopes of a greater outreach as the program grows.
The popular Trinity Hymnal recommended by the General Assembly has been reprinted. This edition
contains the same 750 hymns, Psalter, Westminster Confession of Faith included in earlier editions plus the
Shorter Catechism. More than 113,000 hymnals have been sold to date.
Your prayers and support are needed to enable us to continue to develop our GCP program. There is
nothing available like this that is uniquely Presbyterian and consistent with the PCA's confessional position.
The PCA was able to contribute only $100,000 of its budgeted $175,000 in 1979. Because the
contributions from the two denominations are used basically to meet salaries and because both churches'
contributions were down in 1979, much needed editorial staff was somewhat curtailed. This meant that the
projected rate of new program development was not realized. However, the sales continued to increase in 1979
thus enabling the production of the programs mentioned above.
The GCP Board of Directors is made up of 12 members, six from the PCA and six from the OPC. They
are elected by each denomination’s Christian Education Committee. They are:
Mr. John Spencer (PCA Chairman) Mr. F. Kingsley Elder (OPC)
Mr. Gene Parks (PCA) Mr. Peyton H. Gardner (OPC)
Mr. O. H. Smith (PCA) Mr. Robert Ashlock (OPC)
The Rev. John Reeves (PCA) The Rev. Jim Bosgraf (OPC)
The Rev. Dan Barr (PCA) The Rev. Don Poundstone (OPC)
The Rev. David Bryson (PCA) The Rev. Edmund P. Clowney (OPC)
the following is a summary statement offered to the General Assembly as a partial report of the financial
status of the joint venture, prepared from the annual audit report for its year ending June 30, 1979:
Financial Position:

II. HIGHER EDUCATION
A. Covenant College
Under the present stage of the plan for joint governance of Covenant College by the RPCES and the PCA,
the PCA has the privilege of having six members of the Board of thirty members.
According to the plan for joint governance, both churches were to take two votes for entrance and
continuation in the plan. The RPCES has already taken its second vote (in 1979) in the affirmative. The PCA
General Assembly is being asked to do so this year.
The College experienced a smaller enrollment this year (1979-80) than had been anticipated. This meant
that there had to be a reworking of the budget during this past academic year, since it had been projected on the
basis of incomes from a larger student body than actually attended. This year's number of PCA students

<a id="ga08-p172"></a>
<!-- PAGE ga=8 pdf_page=172 printed_page=null -->

remained fairly constant with the previous year. We need to encourage more of our students to attend Covenant
College, in order to help make Covenant College more closely identified with the PCA as her College.
God has blessed Covenant College with academically and spiritually capable administration and faculty.
With Covenant College's philosophy of purpose and approach to Christian Education, we believe that it offers
much to young people in the area of higher education. it is worthy of our prayers, utilization and financial
support. The young people that are enrolled at the College are being trained to be leaders in all walks of life.
The plan for joint governance calls for a second step, when the PCA may elect nine members to the
Board, as contrasted to its present six. This takes place, either when the PCA sends an equal number of students
to the College as the RPCES does, or contributes $2,500,000 to the Capital and Endowment Funds. As of the
Fall of 1979, the RPCES had 147 students in the College as compared with 67 from the PCA. Thus, if the PCA
would send a larger number of students to the College, the PCA may have a larger voice in the affairs of the
College.
The other route by which a larger voice may be given to the PCA is through the financial route. It should
be reported that to date the PCA is credited with $759,000 toward the $2,500,000 required for entering the
second phase. One of the PCA Board members, Mr. Oliver Smith, Jr. has made a very generous gift to the
College of 11 acres of land in the Knoxville, Tennessee area. The land is appraised at over $760,000 Mr.
Smith has asked that the College hold the land for a period of time, since it is anticipated that it will increase in
value. When it is sold, the proceeds are to be used as an endowment to insure the operation of the chapel. The
gift is to serve as a living memorial to Dr. Marion Barnes, the former President of the College, and Dr. Luder
Whitlock, who introduced Covenant College to Mr. Smith.
The College continues to labor under financial strain. A challenge gift of $250,000 has been offered to
the College. The attention of our people is called to this opportunity to give to the College, and to assist it to
obtain this gift. Presently, PCA support comes from either individuals or local churches. The College is not in
the Assembly’s budget. Therefore, we would encourage our members to consider support for the College.
III. CHRISTIAN DAY SCHOOLS
Because of the increasing interest among PCA members in the Christian Day School movement and
because many PCA churches presently operate such institutions, the CE/P Committee has established a
sub-committee on Christian Day Schools. It is hoped that the Committee can be a helpful resource to those
churches either in a present program or those projecting future schools.
IV. THEOLOGICAL EDUCATION
A. The PCA’s Program for Candidates for the Gospel Ministry
For three consecutive years the General Assembly has endorsed the approach to theological training of
the candidates for the gospel ministry known as the 3 + I years and 2 + 2 training program. These two
approaches with suggested models are detailed in the minutes of the seventh General Assembly, (1979)
(pp. 190. 191). The 22-Man Sub-Committee consisting of elected representatives continues to serve the
Assembly until at least seven presbyteries have submitted plans which have been approved as meeting the PCA
requirements.
We continue to maintain the position of not endorsing seminaries on the Assembly level but rather have
concentrated on working with presbyteries to develop their own program of theological training, using
whatever institutions may be acceptable both to the presbytery and the candidate involved.
Thus tar, the 22-Man Committee has approved four plans of theological training from four presbyteries;
Central Florida, Evangel, Warrior and Western Carolinas. If the Assembly completes the constitutional action
on the proposed requirement of the one year of licensure, each presbytery at that time will be required to
develop a plan which will be submitted to the Certification Committee for approval.
We encourage the other presbyteries to follow the example of the four listed above in order to facilitate
their training of ministerial candidates.
At the end of the 1978 statistical year, the Stated Clerk's office reported that there were 159 candidates
distributed throughout our presbyteries in training for the gospel ministry in the PCA.
B. Continuing Education for Ministers
The PCA through CE/P continues to present the annual continuing education program for its ministers.
The March 1979 Conference was held in Greenville, S.C. and the March 1980 conference was held in
Birmingham, AL. Location for the March 1981 conference will be announced later by the CE/P office.
C. NAPARC Theological Education Program
The Coordinator of CE/P and the Stated Clerk have represented the PCA in the NAPARC Conference on
Theological Education. The last major NAPARC Conference dealt extensively with "ministerial dropouts ".
In light of things learned from that Conference, we believe the approach of the PCA's3 + I years and/or2 + 2
years program will be of much help to alleviate frustrations and dropouts among the candidates.

### V. THE WOMEN IN THE CHURCH

A. Love Gift
Once agaip a special word of commendation belongs to the local WIC for the generous support of the
Love Gift. In 1979 it was directed toward church building in Mexico and Ecuador. Their total contribution
amounted to slightly over $40,000. While this also increased the local churches reported going to World
Missions, the WIC groups were the catalyst in this major undertaking.
The CE/P Committee and the entire Assembly are continually grateful for the contribution of service
demonstrated by local WIC's.
B. Advisory Sub-Committee
Six ladies from throughout the denomination serve as an advisory subcommittee for the WIC ministry.
They meet quarterly and host an annual meeting for the presidents of the presbyteries WIC organizations. The

<a id="ga08-p173"></a>
<!-- PAGE ga=8 pdf_page=173 printed_page=null -->

conference for presidents was held at Stone Mountain, Ga. in March of 1980. Three-fourths of our presbyteries
were represented.
The present Advisory Sub-Committee is made up of:
Mrs. Sue Fowler (Tennessee Valley)
Mrs. Emalyn Spencer (Evangel)
mr . Ginger Blaylock (Louisiana)
Mrs. Iola Magee (Grace)
Mrs. Emily Russell (Western Carolinas)
Mrs. Bea Annis (Southern Florida)
C. Training Programs
A brief slide/tape training film for local WIC’s is being prepared by the CE/P staff and will soon be made
available to the Church.
VI. FAMILY MINISTRIES
A. Local Seminars
The CE/P staff includes family and marriage seminar programs for local churches . Several were
conducted in 1979. Believing there is a growing need to minister within families, the CE/P has continued to
encourage local churches in their own family ministries.
B. Family Conference
The highlight of the year is the annual PCA Family Conference held at Covenant College. The 1979
Conference attracted families from twelve states during the July week. The 1980 Conference is scheduled for
Covenant College July 14-18, 1980. Dr. Jay Adams, Dr. Palmer Robertson and Rev Terry Gyger will be the
featured speakers.
The purpose of the Conference is to give PCA families from across the Church an opportunity for fun and
fellowship, as well as study in the Scriptures. Full programs for children, youth and adults are available
July 13-17, 1981 has been set aside as 1981 Conference date.
VII. MEDIA
A. Denominational Magazine
The Assembly assigned to the CE/P Committee the task of publishing the official denominational
magazine. It is known as The PCA Messenger. The publication is financed from the CE/P budget and mailed
monthly to the homes of PCA members. With some 27,000 on the present mailing list, nearly 90% of the PCA
families receive the Messenger.
The purpose of the Messenger, is to keep the grassroots of the PCA informed about the news of the
denomination. This involves reporting the programs, actions and projections of the permanent committees and
sub-committees of the Assembly.
The Messenger is a key to presenting the whole work of the Church. It is our hope that as the families
receive the monthly publication, it will be read thereby generating prayer and support for the Assembly's
programs.
Names to be included on the mailing list should be mailed to the CE/P office. Box 39, Decatur. Ga.
30031. Because this project is financed from CE/P budget, there is no subscription fee.
B. Other Studies
The CE/P Committee is continuing to study the need for further programs in the area of news. Because
the PCA is a national church there appears to be an increasing need to expand the news coverage of the PCA.
VIII. FINANCES
The support of the CE/P budget continues to bring blessings, frustration, challenges and humility. The
contributions for 1979 see-sawed each month. The year-end total was 74% of the budget, the same percentage
as 1978.
While God in His providence enabled us to develop some new programs and materials, to expand the
Messenger to a monthly schedule, cut backs were made because the budget was not fully subscribed.
Some have been consistently faithful as individuals and churches with generous support. This has
brought much encouragement. We have had to deal with disappointments also in not being able to meet the
approved budget. We have sought to be good stewards with your contributions. We did not attempt to spend
beyond the contributions.
There are so many needs that we are aware of across the Church in numerous areas. We only regret that
we have not been able to respond to requests for assistance in certain areas of program materials and training.
Please pray with us that 1980 will show an increase in prayers and support throughout the Church and that
the needed staff and programs will be made possible by your help
We offer the following proposed 1981 budget not as our attempt to project realistic goals for that year but
in hopes that God will enable us to move forward to meet the challenges and needs of local churches and
presbyteries.
(Clerk's note: the text of the budget is found in the report of the Sub-Committee on Stewardship
Ministries.)
IX. OTHERS
Because 60% to 75% of each local church's program falls under the umbrella of Christian Education, the
needs are many. The Assembly's CE/P Committee is constantly planning ways of offering more help to the
local church.
Materials, seminars, equipment and programs are needed on a week-to-week schedule. Some things can
be done locally. Outside help is needed for many others No one church has the capability of developing a full

<a id="ga08-p174"></a>
<!-- PAGE ga=8 pdf_page=174 printed_page=null -->

program consistent with the PCA's position and needs. The Committee continues to be receptive to suggestions
and requests for assistance from throughout the denomination.
We want to develop some basic Bible study materials that will disciple new Christians into the faith and
the Church. We are in hopes of developing more programs and resources in the area of audio-visual materials.
Presently, we offer quite a storehouse of tape programs through the Mt. Olive Tape Library.
Over 100 churches and civic groups used the Assembly's slide/tape program on Abortion. More than 300
local W IC's and churches used the slide/tape program in church building in Mexico and Ecuador.
We must not miss out on the importance of the electronic media and means of evangelization and
education in God’s Word. Please pray for us as the Committee sets priorities. We believe that God has given us
unique opportunities and open doors. You can help us by making your needs known, and by supporting our
work.

#### X. RECOMMENDATIONS:

- **a)** Within the 50 states of the USA to Mission to the United States;
- **b)** Outside the 50 states of the USA to Mission to the World.

Appendix F
REPORT OF THE COMMITTEE ON MISSION TO THE
UNITED STATES TO THE EIGHTH GENERAL ASSEMBLY OF
THE PRESBYTERIAN CHURCH IN AMERICA
INTRODUCTION
Christ promised the twelve, through their spokesman, Peter, that He would build His Church and that the
onslaughts of Hell would not overcome it. Ours is the comfort of the Scriptures as we labor in our assigned task
of witnessing to America and gathering the fruit of our labor into a Church that is faithful to the apostolic faith
and discipline.
In the year 1979, God graciously blessed the Presbyterian Church in America with both spiritual and
physical growth. To that end He used all parts of the body of which your Committee was one part. We cannot
evaluate spiritual growth easily, except as physical development is an expression of inner vitality, but we can in
this report tell the Assembly some of the visible accomplishments.
A YEAR’S HIGHLIGHTS
1. The year has witnessed some change and growth in the administrative staff. An organizational chart,
approved by the Committee in December, provided spaces for an Assistant Coordinator for Special Ministries,
an Assistant Coordinator for Church Development and a Director of Financial Programs (including 5 in 5). The
Rev. Dwight Linton was chosen to fill the position as Assistant Coordinator for Special Ministries. He has
taken the place of The Rev. David Clowney who was Director of the Urban, Poor and Minority Ministries. Mr.
Allen Duble was chosen as Director of Financial Programs, working part-time at least through July 1980, and
he has taken the place of The Rev. Donald Graham who was Director of 5 in 5. The Committee wishes to
acknowledge to the Assembly its gratitude for the selfless and fruitful service of both Mr. Graham and Mr.
Clowney.
2. In March 1979, we had twenty-nine men under full or partial support and had committed the
Committee to begin the support of eight other ministries in the first six months of 1979. In March of 1980, when
this report was written, we were committed to the support of thirty-four men or ministries. Nine of these were
special ministries (UPM). We are in the process of planting churches in 19 states. Some of these ministries,
outside the bounds of any Presbytery, receive no financial support from the Assembly's Committee, but they
do receive guidance, encouragement and the prayer support generated by the M-US office.
3. While evangelism and church planting are primary, the development of the Church is also accomp
lished by helping those evangelicals confronted by the dead-end street of denominational liberalism to find a
new and mutually beneficial relationship within the PCA. Much time has been spent by your Committee in
representing the PCA before these people, many of them in the north, and only now are we beginning to see
positive results. We encourage the Assembly to pray for those in the midst of their struggles.
4. We believe that the worth of your Committee’s efforts should be'judged by the fruit of developed
churches or new additions to the rolls of our Presbyteries. Through the last General Assembly, M-US had made
an average expenditure of $9,500.00 in each of forty church planting efforts which had come to self-support
and organization as particular churches. In the one year, 1978, each of those forty churches returned to
Assembly causes, besides other benevolent giving, an average of $3,250. In 1979 at least 13 churches that were
nurtured by the Assembly’s Committee were added to our presbyteries.
5. Three hundred and three churches contributed to the Assembly’s M-US program in 1979. These
annual contributions ranged from $5.00 to a high of $49,000. Seventy-one percent of the Committee’s
expenditures came from churches.
6. If the 1979 expenditures of your M-US Committee were put in “ apple pie order," the division of the
pie would look like this: Organizing Pastors 39%, Administrative and Office 22%, Urban-Poor-Minorities
20%, Miscellaneous one-time projects (Mobile chapels, computer, etc.) 15%, Assembly and Stewardship4%.
7. Perhaps a major shift in direction for M-US is toward regional development of the Church with the
intent of establishing new presbyteries or strengthening those that are as yet unable to capitalize on their
opportunities to develop the Church within their own boundaries. While we seek to strengthen the Pacific
Presbytery as a whole, we see its territories as distinct regions to be developed into new presbyteries with more
limited and practical boundaries. The example of a region in which we have no presbytery but where much
concentrated development is required is New England. We are now in the process of developing an
experienced church planting team of New Englanders, tapping the expertise of those already in the area, the
student manpower of those at Gordon-Conwell and the financial ability of individuals including a friendly
Foundation — all under the heading of PCA Ministries in New England. This will be our new MINE from
which to extract spiritual wealth as the churches of the Assembly hold the ropes.
8. Following the approval of the Campus Ministries Manual by the Seventh General Assembly, the
M-US Committee entered into association with one presbytery for the shared support of a campus ministry.
Your Committee has also endorsed 4 candidates for the military chaplaincy. The list of Chaplains in the
minutes of the Seventh General Assembly remains unchanged.
9. The reports of our Special Ministries Sub-Committee (UPM) and the 5 in 5 Campaign are attached as
a part of this report.
RECOMMENDATIONS
1. That Sunday, November 23, 1980, be set aside as a special day of prayer and thanksgiving for the

Season to go toward the work of our Ministries in New England, that place where the first American

Appendix A
REPORT ON REQUEST OF THE SEVENTH GENERAL ASSEMBLY
FOR AN ADDITION TO THE M-US POLICY MANUAL
“ TRAINING OF ORGANIZING PASTORS”
(Adopted as amended by the Eighth General Assembly)
Explanation: The Committee of Commissioners on Mission to the United States made a report to the Seventh
General Assembly, which report was approved, regarding the revision of the existing Mission to the United
States Policy Manual.
Background:
1. The Fourth General Assembly in 1976 approved a Policy Manual submitted by the Permanent
Committee on Mission to the U.S. However, the Committee of Commissioners on M-US made the following
recommendation which was adopted: "That the Policy Manual include a section establishing guidelines for all
non-ordained personnel working for Mission to the United States in mission churches. Example: Seminary
students on clinical year leaves."
2. The Permanent Committee then petitioned the Fifth Assembly in 1977: "The Committee be granted
an extension of one year on the Policy Manual dealing with the establishing of guidelines for all non-ordained
personnel working for Mission to the United States in mission churches. Example: Seminary students on
clinical leaves.' ’ The Assembly granted the request.
3. Since the Sixth General Assembly in 1978 met at a time of transition for the Permanent Committee,
that Committee of Commissioners asked for another year to prepare a revised M-US Policy Manual. The
Committee of Commissioners in the Sixth Assembly placed that request on the Assembly floor, but in doing so
added substantially to the assignment originated two years before: "That the M-US Committee be granted
another year to prepare a revised M-US Policy Manual, and that a new M-US Policy Manual including a
training program for organizing pastors be presented to all presbytery M-US committee chairmen for their
suggestions before the final draft is prepared, and that the Permanent Committee present the revised Policy
Manual to the Seventh General Assembly.
Grounds: A real need felt by some of the organizing pastors for more training.
4. The Committee on Mission to the United States called a meeting of those who represented M-US
Committees in our various presbyteries in the spring of 1979 and requested that they work over documents to be
presented to the Seventh General Assembly, including one on Training of Organizing Pastors and one on
Guidelines on Unordained Personnel. The final drafts were sent to presbytery M-US committees for comment
and then presented to the Seventh General Assembly. The Committee of Commissioners made the following
recommendations regarding these two documents which recommendations were adopted:
5. That Recommendation 4 of M-US, to approve the “ Training Manual for Organizing Pastors" be
replaced by the following:
a. That Attachment A (p. 198. entitled “ Training Manual for Organizing Pastors" be approved
with the title deleted, and included as part of the operational policy manual of M-US.
Adopted.
b. That Attachments A. "Progress Report from Organizing Pastor", and B, “ Progress Report
from Treasurer" (p. 201), be received as information and be regarded as operational
procedures.
Adopted.
c . That M-US prepare a revised policy manual for approval by the Eighth General Assembly for
information and an operation manual to be in conformity with the policy manual. These two
manuals shall be extractions from all policy previously approved by all General Assemblies.
Any changes in future operational procedure shall be submitted to the Committee of Commissioners of M-US yearly for review. Any procedures that are found not to be in conformity with
the Policy Manual are to be brought to the attention of the General Assembly for consideration
and action.
Grounds:
1) There has been an increasing proliferation of documents created by past General Assembly actions as policy, of which some are policy and others are merely operational
directives which have been approved as policy; and
2) The Minutes of the Sixth General Assembly, 6-51, Recommendation A, page 76, call
fora training program and not a manual, but attachment ais neither a training manual
nor a training program. Adopted.
7. The Attachment B (p. 203) the "Guidelines for Lay Ministries” be approved as presented.
Adopted.
Thus, the mandate carried over from the Fourth Assembly, to add a section to the M-US manual on lay
workers was satisfied, but the requirement to fit into the manual a section on the training of organizing pastors
was not yet satisfied. It is the Permanent Committee's belief that the Assembly has, from 1976, only wanted to
add a brief section to the excellent Policy Manual accepted in that year, and not to require the Committee to
undertake a complete rewrite. This issue may have become confused as the original request was translated from
Assembly to Assembly.

<a id="ga08-p178"></a>
<!-- PAGE ga=8 pdf_page=178 printed_page=null -->

Therefore, the Permanent Committee on Mission to the United States has taken the concepts on training
approved by the Seventh Assembly after review by presbytery M-US committees, and presents them to the
Eighth Assembly with the request that they be inserted under the section titled “ The Organizing Pastor"
instead of the present very brief section C. At the same time, the very brief sub-section 4 under A should be
eliminated. The section to be added would then read as follows:
C. Training of Organizing Pastors
1. General — In addition to natural attributes and spiritual gifts (A, I & 2 above), an organizing pastor
will benefit from professional preparation for his call. Specialized preparation should begin as soon as he
decides to minister in church planting and should continue after his appointment.
2. Goals — Organizing pastor is expected to develop further his knowledge and skills in areas
essential to church planting, examples of which are the ability to train others in personal witnessing,
discipleship, leadership training and skills, and organizational skills.
3. Methods — Continuing education for the organizing pastor is a joint responsibility of all parties:

- **a.** Self-study — whether through formal courses, home study, periodicals or seminars, the organizing pastor should continue studying subjects related to his call.
- **b.** Personal contact — guidance, counsel and instruction should be provided by the entire PCA church planting team. This team could include:

1) The Coordinator of M-US and his staff
2) The presbytery's Minister of Church Growth
3) The chairman of the presbytery M-US committee
4) a senior minister and his session ina nearby church to whom the organizing pastor is
assigned for assistance and evaluation.
c. Seminars
I) The organizing pastor should attend available training seminars sponsored by M-US or
other organizations.
d. Clinical Ministry
I) as indicated on an individual basis, the committee may spend funds to place a man in a
clinical ministry or supervised field work.
4. Evaluating Accomplishments
a. The Organizing Pastor will be evaluated by the Mission to the United States according to his
diligence in the various aspects of his ministry, his accomplishment of the goals and
objectives outlined in this manual and the specific measurable goals for church growth
mutually arrived at by the various parties involved. In addition, there will be continued
evaluation of the location where the organizing pastor is ministering. It is possible that the
M-US Committee may in some instances determine over time that a certain area is resistant to
the Gospel and that that is the major factor why certain church growth goals have not been
reached. The M-US Committee may, however, still be convinced that the organizing pastor is
diligent and qualified so that, instead of eventually terminating his financial support, it may
rather move him to a different place of service.
b. The function of the Coordinator and his staff is to support, encourage and aid according to the
organizing pastor's need. But he also has the responsibility to report to the Committee at
regular intervals the progress or the lack of it. To evaluate progress, tools must be employed:
1) a report form will be completed by the organizing pastor at least on a quarterly basis and
forwarded to the Coordinator with a copy to the office his presbytery may designate.
2) A companion financial report form will be completed by the organizing pastor or the
session of the congregation served by the organizing pastor.
3) A semi-annual report of progress will be made by at least one member of the church
planting team. (Cf., Par. G., 3., & b, above).
4) An annual report from the General Assembly M-US staff or a Committee member based
on a personal visit to the area of ministry.
c. The Committee on Mission to the United States will:
1) Receive a summary progress report from the Coordinator on each organizing pastor
twice in each year (usually in March and September), and take any appropriate action.
2) Determine any resulting changes in the terms of the “ Call" to the organizing pastor, and
send revised call to the pastor, his presbytery, and when applicable, to the Session he
serves.

<a id="ga08-p179"></a>
<!-- PAGE ga=8 pdf_page=179 printed_page=null -->

Appendix B
REPORT TO THE EIGHTH GENERAL ASSEMBLY
BY
THE FIVE IN FIVE CAMPAIGN
Prepared by E. Allen Duble
Fathers and Brethren:
on the day that i write this brief report to the eighth General Assembly i am struck by contrasts. In the
morning i met with a group of four men whoa re convinced of the Lord’s leading to step out on faith and claim a
piece of inner-city property which offers all of the special multi-purpose requirements of an urban ministry,
including day care, a thrift store, a youth ministry and more. The funds needed in 40 days are $134,000, yet the
group meeting is a modest 25 persons of extraordinary vision. Can 5-in-5 help?
In the afternoon I talked on the telephone to an organizing pastor whom M-US supported at $500 per
month to assist him on the field three years ago. he meets ina school with a group of 650 people who have
bought 18'/i acres of land for $100,000 cash, and would seem to be well along the way toward a church
building. However, they have been advised that construction must be underway by late summer before the
School Board will renew their option on the meeting facilities. This group has found that even with their
property the banks will not make a long-term loan because of today's financial climate.
On this day with barely 12 days of service for this acting director of 5-in-5, it becomes painfully clear that
indeed the harvest of opportunities is ripe but the General Assembly’s visionary goal is painfully short of the
mark and has become greatly diminished even in its ability to perform, due to the inflationary crisis in our
economy. It would be a major discouragement if it were not for our Glorious Provider who touches the hearts of
His people.
On December 31, 1979, pledges on record in this office from 109 churches and 114 individuals to the
5-in-5 program total $1,754,123. Receipts to December 31, 1979, are $454,874. (It should be noted in passing
that attrition does occur in pledges and, in fact, one pledging church has been closed at the time of this report.)
How will we then provide the 5 million dollar revolving loan fund that has been envisioned by the
General Assembly? It will only be accomplished by the hearty and sacrificial giving of those churches and
individuals whom God has prospered with faith and resources.
And how will we sustain and safeguard such a fund from the inroads of inflation? Will there be anything
left to revolve?
it is to this last question that i plan to focus my attention and to request the counsel of a number of the
leaders of the denomination in the weeks ahead. I will hope to have some special recommendations concerning
that question by the time of General Assembly.
RECOMMENDATIONS:
1. To acknowledge with thanks the tireless efforts of Donald C. Graham in his work to build the 5-in-5
program to the level it has reached in December, 1979.
2. To reaffirm the idea and the goal of the 5-in-5 program (on the basis of one dollar-per month-per
member-over-five-years-asking).
3. To encourage the churches who have not yet made their pledge to this special assembly-wide program
to do so prayerfully and promptly in order to facilitate the building of the church.
4. To affirm the formation of a special 5-in-5 taskforce as a sub-committee of the Mission to the U.S.
Committee to achieve the 5 million dollar goal promptly and to structure a strategy to safeguard it from the
erosion of inflation and the debilitating influences of the economy.
5. To consider the following change in the 5-in-5 program:
Faced with the effect of skyrocketing inflation on 5-in-5 funds, we recommend that the fund be administered as
an endowment, with loans for new church development being made from the interest received on these
investment funds. This would, we believe, help safeguard the value of funds contributed against great financial
erosion, and fulfill the original intention of the donors.

<a id="ga08-p180"></a>
<!-- PAGE ga=8 pdf_page=180 printed_page=null -->

Appendix C
REPORT TO THE EIGHTH GENERAL ASSEMBLY
BY
THE SUB-COMMITTEE ON URBAN, POOR, & MINORITY MINISTRIES
Prepared by Dwight Linton
(As amended and approved by the Eighth General Assembly)
INTRODUCTION
In the early days of His ministry, our Lord quoted the book of Isaiah to explain what He had come to do,
saying, “ The Spirit of the Lord is upon me, because He anointed me to preach the Gospel to the poor. He has
sent me to proclaim release to the captives, and recovery of sight to the blind, to set free those who are
downtrodden, to proclaim the favorable year of the Lord.' ’ (Luke 4:18-19) Later we find Jesus — amazed at the
faith of a Roman Centurion — predicting that, " . . . many shall come from east and west, and recline at table
with Abraham, Isaac, and Jacob in the kingdom of Heaven . . .” (Matthew 8:11)
In these two passages, we meet the challenge that our Lord has laid on us and we are amazed as we see
how His words, spoken two thousand years ago, describe so accurately our present situation. Our modem
society has produced the “ inner city” of today with its frustrating problems and we are called to minister at that
point. God, in His wisdom, has brought millions from the east and the west to our very doorstep and it is our
calling to invite them in to the feast. In many cases, these peoples of diverse cultures whom God has brought to
our land have settled in the inner city and are the poor to whom we must minister. Whatever the situation, we
who have the knowledge of the true Gospel must share it with them, recognizing the diversity in life-style and
practice that will occur while holding firmly to the unity of our precious faith. We must pray that our beloved
church will catch the vision of this mission field on our doorstep, and in obedience to our Lord, carry out this
ministry.
THE YEAR IN REVIEW
Since the last General Assembly the UMP Committee has met 4 times, and has reported its progress to the
M-US Committee.
It has also:
— Called the Sam Lings to work as church planters in New York City among the Chinese in Queens.
— Worked out an agreement with Wycliffe, whereby Claude and Pat Sharpe can work among the Sea
Island people along the coast of Georgia and South Carolina.
—Called the Jerry Douglases to join the Levies in the work among the Navajos in Arizona.
— Attempted to help the CE Committee to raise support for the denomination's Scholarship Fund in order
to make funds available to minority candidates. At present, sufficient funds have not been raised to meet
the needs of those who have applied and been approved.
— Received with regret Rev. David Clowney's resignation as part-time director of UPM, effective May
1, 1980. His duties have been assumed by the new Assistant Coordinator of M-US, The Rev. Dwight
Linton.
— Proposed and received approval from M-US fora revision of its structure and a procedure for
appointing its members. The members of the new UPM Sub-Committee are: Charles Dunahoo, Earl H.
Russell, Jr., Manuel Salabarria, Jim Hughes, Mitchell Hall, W. B. (Pete) Gibbs, Robert D. Wilcox,
Charles C. Cox, Jr., John Seery, Robert Koren.
—Continued to develop its strategy proposal for presentation to the 1981 General Assembly, and entered
into a contract with the Center for Urban Theological Studies in Philadelphia to help develop this
proposal.
—Continued the supervision of the current UPM workers.
— Formulated a new approach to the work in New Jersey which will be an advisory board called
“ Mission to Essex County.'' This mission will provide a more efficient way of achieving our goals in that
it will incorporate leadership training and church planting in one mission with the oversight of leaders in
the immediate area. This will be done under the supervision of the M-US.
—Called Michael J. Gross to work with the Navajo Bible School in Window Rock, Arizona.
— concurred ipa call to Rev. Kevin CarT to fill the pastorate of the Navajo Church in Window Rock,
Arizona.

Appendix E
DRAFT CONSTITUTION
REFORMED JOINT COMMISSION ON
CHAPLAINS AND MILITARY PERSONNEL
(Italics indicates changes, many of which were requested by the seventh General Assembly.)
ARTICLE I — NAME
The name of this organization shall be the Presbyterian and Reformed Joint Commission on Chaplains
and Military Personnel.
ARTICLE II — PURPOSES
The Commission is an agent of its member denominations (not an ecclesiastical commission in the
technical sense), created by them to assist in carrying out their ministries to members of the Armed Forces and
other institutions. Since the primary structure for such ministries is through formal chaplaincies, the principal
activity and concern of the Commission shall be involved with chaplains.
In carrying out its mission, the Commission functions in the following ways:
1. By maintaining liaison with the appropriate contact point of each member denomination, and through
their various presbyteries, to:
(a) provide current information regarding criteria, policies and procedures for the appointment of
ministers as chaplains.
(b) provide a technical service to the Presbyteries by recommending qualified candidates to the
chaplaincy.
2. By maintaining cooperative relationships with the Armed Forces Chaplain Board and the leadership of
military and other institutional chaplaincies by:
(a) certifying to the proper agencies the ecclesiastical endorsements and approvals granted by the
member denominations through their various presbyteries.
(b) serving as a representative body for problems referred by or concerning chaplains, both
individually and collectively.
3. By maintaining contact and liaison with individual chaplains serving in the Armed Forces and other
institutions through regular reports, newsletters, written correspondence, and persona] visits as authorized by
the Commission. Ordinarily personal visits shall be made only in consultation with the Presbytery involved.
4. By establishing and maintaining methods of liaison with individual congregations of the member
denominations to assist them in providing adequate ministry to their members while they are separated from the
particular church during periods in the Armed Forces and other institutions.
5. By keeping member denominations informed on significant developments, trends, issues and
problems concerning chaplains and members of the Armed Forces and other institutions and to report annually
on the activity of the Commission to each member denomination through the proper agencies.
* (6. is deleted and Paragraph 7 is moved up to become Paragraph 6.)
6. By assisting the Presbyteries in the promotion of the ministry of the chaplaincy to the member
denominations and their particular churches.
ARTICLE HI — MEMBERSHIP
The Commission is made up of the following member denominations:
1. Orthodox Presbyterian Church
2. Presbyterian Church in America
3. Reformed Presbyterian Church, Evangelical Synod
Any member denomination may withdraw from the Commission by act of its General Assembly/Synod.
Any denomination seeking membership in the Commission shall:
1. Submit a letter application for membership to the Commission no later than 1 January of the year in
which it desires membership.
2. Be approved by vote of all current member denominations at their General Assembly/Synod.
Each member denomination or its responsible committee shall elect three representatives to serve as
voting members of the Commission, with qualifications and terms to be set by the member denominations.
ARTICLE IV — INCORPORATION
The Commission shall be incorporated under the Corporation laws of the State of Colorado relating to
non-profit, religious corporations.
ARTICLE V — RULES OF ORDER
The Commission shall regulate its own proceedings in accordance with its Articles of Incorporation,
Constitution, By-Laws, and Robert Rules of Order (current edition). ** (2 ,3 ,4 ,5 and 6 under V are deleted.)
ARTICLE VI — FINANCIAL SUPPORT
The financial support of the Commission shall be primarily the responsibility of the member denomina
tions, and shall be on a fair share basis, as recommended by the Commission. Each denomination will be
responsible for all expenses incurred by its representatives at any meetings of the Commission or its
sub-committees. Each denomination shall be asked to contribute a set amount per year per full time/active duty
chaplain endorsed by the Commission. Each chaplain endorsed, both full and part-time, active duty and

<a id="ga08-p185"></a>
<!-- PAGE ga=8 pdf_page=185 printed_page=null -->

reserve, shall be asked to contribute an appropriate amount per year, if able. Amounts of contributions will be
recommended by the Commission for the approval of the member denominations annually. In addition, the
commissions h il be free to receive donations from individuals and churches, both within and without the
member denominations.
ARTICLE VII — STAFF
The Commission shall have as its chief operating officer an Executive Secretary, and may hire such other
personnel as it may determine.
ARTICLE VIII — AMENDMENTS
This constitution may be amended only by written submission of proposed amendment by the Commission to each of the member denominations and by subsequent approval of each member denomination at their
annual General Assembly/Synod.
DRAFT BY-LAWS
ARTICLE I — THE OFFICERS
The Commission shall have the following officers to be elected annually: chairman, vice-chairman, and
secretary.
ARTICLE II — MEETINGS
The Commission shall meet each year at a time fixed by the Commission. Special meetings of the
Commission may be called by the chairman by petition of at least one third of the membership, representing at
least two member denominations.
ARTICLE III — FINANCIAL POLICY AND PROCEDURES
The financial policy and procedures shall be proposed by the Executive Secretary and approved by the
Commission. The fiscal year for the Commission shall be the calendar year.
ARTICLE IV — ELECTION AND EMPLOYMENT OF STAFF
The executive staff of the Commission shall consist of the Executive Secretary and such other members
as the Commission may from time to time authorize. Members of the executive staff normally shall be elected
for a term of one year. Should the Commission desire, for cause, to terminate the services of such staff, or such
staff desire to resign, six days notice shall be given, unless waived by mutual agreement. Non-executive
personnel shall be employed by the Executive Secretary in accordance with the personnel policy approved by
the Commission.
ARTICLE V — DUTIES OF EXECUTIVE SECRETARY
The Executive Secretary shall function as the executive of the Commission and shall give supervision to
all other personnel. He shall have responsibility and authority for the general direction and oversight of the
total program of the Commission. He shall be responsive and accountable to the policies and actions of the
Commission at all times. He shall function as the treasurer for the Commission.
ARTICLE VI — QUORUM
For either regular or special meetings of the Commission, a quorum shall consist of more than one-half of
the representative members of the Commission, with at least one representative from each member denomination present.
ARTICLE VII — AMENDMENTS
The Bylaws of the Commission may be amended at any regular meeting of the Commission by a
three-fourths vote of the voting representatives present, provided that written notice of such action shall have
been sent out in connection with the notice of the meeting at least 10 days prior to the date of the meeting.
Appendix F

SUPPLEMENTAL REPORT OF THE COMMITTEE ON THE
MISSION TO THE UNITED STATES TO THE
EIGHTH GENERAL ASSEMBLY OF THE
PRESBYTERIAN CHURCH IN AMERICA
REPORT OF THE PRESBYTERIAN AND REFORMED COMMISSION ON CHAPLAINS AND
MILITARY PERSONNEL
Fathers and Brethren:
Our newly formed Commission on Chaplains met on 31 July, 1979, at the Park Hill Orthodox
Presbyterian Church, Denver, Colorado, with all nine members present representing the Orthodox Presbyterian Church, the Presbyterian Church in America, and the Reformed Presbyterian Church, Evangelical

<a id="ga08-p186"></a>
<!-- PAGE ga=8 pdf_page=186 printed_page=null -->

Synod. Changes in our Constitution and By-laws, passed last year by the General Assemblies of the OPC and
PCA, were approved. As now revised, the Constitution and By-laws are presented again this year for approval.
Your Chairman attended the October 1979, and March 1980, meetings of the Ecclesiastical Endorsing
Agents in Washington, D.C. with the Armed Forces Chaplains Board and the Chiefs of Chaplains and their
staffs. We wrestled with the critical shortage of Chaplains in the reserve components, particularly Army,
which needs some 900 additional reserve chaplains; mobilization plans for emergencies; continuing pressure
from several religious sects seeking to place chaplains in the military (thus far avoided by adhering to
established educational requirements); morale problems in the military, especially enlisted and junior grade
officers, arising from low pay scales; shortages of minority group chaplains. We invite our ministers to
consider the reserve chaplaincy program. Obligations include weekly or monthly drills, two weeks active duty
per year, all with pay. Congregations should, however, be fully informed of any reserve obligations of their
pastors.
Endorsements. All endorsements to the chaplaincy originate at presbytery level, and all information required
on the Department of Defense Form 2088 (attached to this report) must be forwarded through the Assembly's
M-US Committee to our chaplaincy commission which provides formal endorsement to the Armed Forces
Chaplains Board and the Chiefs of Chaplains.
For your chairman this has been a year of change, challenge and hard work after 27 years of ministry in
Colorado Springs, service as a chaplain in W. W. II and the Korean War, and other pastorates. But we expect
soon to have office facilities when our home in Buena Vista, Colorado is completed. Please note our address
and phone number at the end of this report.
During the coming year we will be sharpening up our endorsement procedures and exploring a concern
for chaplain ministries to other than the military, i.e. chaplains for police, hospitals, veterans administration,
national park service, nursing and retirement homes, industry. We will be guided in part by any declaration of
need from our three denominations.
Again, we urge sessions, presbyteries and members of our churches to maintain contact with our
chaplains and their families and with members on active duty in the military. Inflation is hurting us all. but
especially our military.
Respectfully submitted,
William B. Leonard, Jr., Executive Secretary,
PRESBYTERIAN & REFORMED COMMISSION
ON CHAPLAINS AND MILITARY PERSONNEL
33625 Wapiti Circle
Buena Vista, Colorado 81211
(303)395-8585

Appendix G
REPORT OF THE COMMITTEE ON MISSION TO THE WORLD
TO THE EIGHTH GENERAL ASSEMBLY
OF THE PRESBYTERIAN CHURCH IN AMERICA
I. DOXOLOGY
In giving this report to the Eighth General Assembly your Committee on Mission to the World would like
to begin by giving praise to God and sincere thanks to the many congregations and individuals who have helped
make possible the accomplishments herein described. ' to god be the Glory, Great Things He Hath Done.
II. OVERVIEW
Since the General Assembly of June 18-22, 1979, and continuing through March 1, 1980, 19 missionaries
have been added to the family of Mission to the World. Others have terminated their service with Mission to the
World. They are listed elsewhere in this report. Of the 19 received 14 will be involved in evangelism and/or
church planting. Seven of these will serve with PCA-only ministries, and 12 will serve with other agencies with
whom we have cooperative agreements. Twenty new missionaries have completed their itineration and have
left for the field since the last General Assembly. The PCA now has church-planting teams in Korea, Taiwan.
Mexico, Ecuador and Portugal and is planting churches with another agency (The Christian Reformed Foreign
Mission Board) in El Salvador. PCA missionaries are involved in church renewal and theological education in
France and in theological education in Mexico and Nigeria. Christ's College in Taiwan, where 10 PCA
missionaries serve on the faculty, continues to be a principal ministry.
The Twelfth Inter-Varsity Student Missions Convention was held at Urhana, Illinois, the week after
Christmas, 1979. Under the leadership of PCA missionary John Kyle, the I-V Director of Missions, Urbana
gathered over 17,000 young people from all over America and several foreign countries. The interest of these
young people in missions was both inspiring and exciting. Many of the delegates visited the PCA/MTW booth,
and almost 100 expressed positive interest in the possibility of serving overseas with MTW. About 25 are
considered excellent prospects. Some of these young people were interested in PCA-only church-planting
opportunities, some in opportunities at Christ's College and some in serving with other agencies in support
roles. It was a most encouraging experience for all those of the MTW staff who attended. MTW was
represented by the Coordinator Paul McKaughan; Kennedy Smartt, Coordinator of Church Relations; Jay
Kyle, Assistant to the Coordinator; Jim Hughes, Director of Personnel; Dr. and Mrs. Donald B. Patterson;
Rev. Henry Schum; and candidate Judy Hurley.
IU. FINANCES
One of the clearest evidences of the blessing of God upon the PCA's world mission operation has been in
the realm of finances. The figures for 1979 show that income was 99.3% of the budget, and expenditures
95.3%. Total income increased 28.4% over 1978. Fifty-nine percent of our missionaries had positive balances
in their support account in spite of the steady increases caused by inflation and devaluation of the dollar. The
other 41% of our missionaries were showing year-end deficits which totaled $47,990.80 for PCA-only
missionaries and $32,175.36 for PCA missionaries serving under cooperative agreements with other agencies.
MTW has in reserve accounts $76,591.65 to cover the deficits of PCA-only missionaries, and $37,256.32 to
cover the deficits of those serving with other agencies. Listed below is a table showing the amount of monthly
support needed of all PCA missionaries as of March 3, 1980;

* Itinerating F Furlough
The Women in the Church Love Gift in 1979 was designated for church buildings in Acapulco and Quito.
The total for this gift came to $40,122.37. An additional $ 15,000 was contributed by an individual in one of our
churches just as fie year ended. These funds are being made available to the teams in Mexico and Ecuador.
Most churches in the PCA are involved in the support of missionaries to some extent with both funds and
prayer. However, some churches are like the Macedonian church that Paul held up before the Corinthians (II
Cor. 8) a san example to be emulated. The extent to which these churches are involved in giving has no
apparent relation to whether they are rich or poor, large or small, city or rural, upper class or lower. Rather, the
depth of their involvement in missions seems to be related to such qualities as leadership, faith, priority,
commitment and direction, They are churches whose vision is turned out instead of in.
In 1979, a rural church of 153 members gave $28,500 for PCA missionary support. A resort area church
with 180 members gave $9,000; a small town church in the deep south, with 77 members, gave about $7,200;
another, with 95 members, gave $10,000. A church of approximately 1800 members in 1979 gave MTW
$142,000 of its almost $200,000 total mission budget; another with 230 members gave $9,700 of its total
missions giving through MTW. One of the exemplary medium-sized churches with 334 members gave
$20,000. It is the commitment and faithfulness of churches like these — and there are many — which have
enabled MTW to grow as it has.
IV. HOME PERSONNEL
Approximately 135 churches in the PCA have regular missions conferences, featuring special missions
speakers, missionaries and candidates. By far, most of the support for MTW comes from these churches.
During the past 12 months MTW Coordinator of Church Relations Kennedy Smartt and MTW Missionary-
Evangelist Jimmy Lyons have conducted conferences in about 80 PCA churches. Twenty-four of these were
first-time conferences. In addition, they have spoken at numerous other gatherings in the interest of world
missions.
Coordinator Paul McKaughan visited MTW work overseas in Korea, Taiwan, the Philippines, Papua
New Guinea and Mexico during the past year, giving counsel, direction, oversight, encouragement and
instruction to our missionaries on the field, as well as gathering information and recommendations for the
permanent Committee. In addition to these trips he has represented the PCA/MTW at meetings of the National
Association of Evangelicals, Evangelical Foreign Missions Association, North American Presbyterian and
Reformed Council, Inter-Varsity Christian Fellowship and the China Consultations.
One of the highlights of last year's General Assembly was the report of The Reverend Jimmy Lyons on
his visit to the refugee centers in Southeast Asia. His slide program, "What Shall the King Say?", has been
shown in over 50 churches, and many have written MTW for help in sponsoring refugees. Since sponsoring is
handled through other agencies, MTW has no precise count on how many churches and how many refugees are
involved. A conservative estimate is 20 churches who have worked with at least 20 families of refugees, but
information on this is still being received in the MTW office. In one case a presbytery (Warrior) pooled their
resources to sponsor four families which they settled in Tuscaloosa, Alabama.
A word of appreciation needs to be expressed in this report for the very fine job accomplished by the staff
of MTW: John McDavid, financial secretary; Jim Hughes, director of personnel; Allan McLean, itineration
secretary; and Jay Kyle, assistant to the coordinator (still on medical leave from overseas duty). These men
have all made tremendous contributions, as has the clerical staff: June Blankenship, Betty Sixto, Shirley
McLean, Anne Kelley, and Charlotte Dean. Frances Jenkins replaced Louise Stout in June as receptionist, and
in February, Helene Spears replaced Mary Lou Bohnsack, Paul McKaughan's very able administrative
assistant. We are grateful for those who have come, but it has been with deep regret that we watched the others
depart.

### V. MISSIONARY FORCE

Space does not permit us to include all the interesting, sobering, challenging and exciting things that have
happened in the lives and ministries of all our missionaries during the past year. In giving just a few highlights it
needs to be made very clear that the life of the missionary on the field, like the lives of all of us who stay home,
is one that is mostly routine, working “ to keep body and soul together,” dealing with problems (practical,
mechanical, spiritual and interpersonal), and struggling to find time for prayer, Bible study and witness. It is a
life threatened by discouragement, challenged by obstacles, and confronted by adversaries. At the same time it
is a ministry appointed by King Jesus, blessed by His Spirit, and enriched by His presence.
Let us share with you just a few episodes from the field — some edited for brevity, some quoted almost
verbatim: (The names of the missionaries are intentionally withheld.)
One missionary reports:
“ Giving at the Harvest Day service at the nearby church was about double that of 1978, reflecting a large
harvest. The pastor here credits the increase to improved farming practices learned through the ag
ricultural program we were able to instigate."
Quotes a letter from one of our Bible translators:
“ I've really had to lean heavily on His strength and He has enabled me to keep up with the teaching even
when I was feeling very weak (malaria). The classes and hours were organized by the Indian leader— 12
women and 15 men and boys from 8:00 a.m. to 5:00 p.m. with (thankfully) two hours for lunch. I’m real

<a id="ga08-p192"></a>
<!-- PAGE ga=8 pdf_page=192 printed_page=null -->

proud of my students! They're an eclectic group — a real challenge and I'm not even a teacher! So I ask God to make me a teacher and an excellent one, for at least this important time He has arranged! We're concentrating on learning what the written/read Word is all about. But one day we interspersed a little arithmetic class for the women because they were having a terrible time knowing how to write 'm,' when to stop making legs! (mmmmm)

... and I thrill to see into God's wonderful power a little bit! He has kept in the Indians' hearts a desire for Him, not just a mere love for me as an old friend (tho I do treasure their love for me). God began a work in 1965 (or before) and is going to finish it. What a thrill that He is letting me in on it still! And how I pray that He will be able to use me right through to the finish of His plan for me with them. And it is only by prayer. We are on the 'front line' together up there where the real battle is!

Another says:

"The month in Celocotitlan God really blessed. It was the first TEE lesson in the new discipleship course of study just translated into Aztec. As the 12 men studied the material in the 4½ hour session, 40 other men and women listened. There were also three adult baptisms. We are all looking forward to the next conference in Tlacosotitlan, the ancient capital of the first Aztecs in our state.

"The Verde Rico Orchard project is progressing. With the new chain saw donated by a church in Mississippi, the men have cleared 21 acres of land. The land has now been planted with corn to keep it from erosion. They will plant thousands of peach trees on it next spring. The produce from these trees will eventually help to support a pastor of their own."

A recent letter from one missionary to his supporters includes a brochure picturing the Mang-duk Church. He says:

"We are particularly proud of that new church. It was started by Mr. Ko. He is a very special friend because he lived in the TB rest home for many months recovering from a bad case of tuberculosis. During that time he was soundly converted and became a most enthusiastic Christian.

"Even before Mr. Ko was completely cured, he started going back and forth to his island home to get a church started there. After recovery he graduated from the Soonchun Bible School and has been serving as a lay minister in the church-planting ministry. The Mang-duk Presbyterian Church was his first effort. You can see the result — one more church established in a village where there was no gospel light before."

Finally, a report of a family's uniting in Christ:

"Recently the father and mother of one of our ministerial students came to our school, after the father had had surgery at the Christian hospital here. I asked our student if his parents were Christians. 'They are pagans,' he replied. The Lord began to work quickly and soon there was a wonderful opportunity to present the gospel to them. They both listened and asked questions at the end. Would they become followers of Jesus Christ? Yes, and right away! We were all amazed. Their son, who stood by silently and who had often presented the gospel to them, was speechless with joy. A few days later the pastor of the church where the parents lived said they threw out their idols and fetishes into the path, causing wonderment in the whole compound. They also wanted to join the communicants' class and become a part of the church. The pastor and I went to the parents' compound and I presented the father and mother into the hands of the pastor and elders for their shepherding. It was a public thing with all the village listening with one ear, so God will get fruit out of that, too. I spent the night in the home of the parents. Next morning the parents gave me a handsome goat which rode home behind the back seat in our car."

The PCA missionary family as of March 1, 1980, now consists of 169 missionaries and missionary candidates serving on or assigned to 23 countries. Seventy-seven of these are PCA-only and 92 are serving with other agencies under cooperative agreements. Since the last report to the General Assembly and continuing through March 1, 1980, the following missionaries completed their terms of service:

Mr. and Mrs. Charles Martin and Mr. and Mrs. Dale Linton resigned to pursue further study for future missionary service; Miss Elizabeth Baldwin resigned; Rev. and Mrs. Archie Moore resigned to return to the States for medical reasons; Miss Brenda Miller, Mr. Ed Smith and Mr. and Mrs. William Owens completed their terms of service.

* Last year’s General Assembly in approving the minutes of the Committee on Mission to the World,
noted an exception in the case of Jakub and Esther Susabda serving in an Indonesian Bible School, because
there was no written cooperative agreement between MTW and the agency under which the Susabdas are
serving. (See Assembly minutes, 1979, p. 128, 7-68, III under "Report").
The Committee on Mission to the World was not asked for an explanation by the Assembly at that time,
but feels such an explanation is needed:
1. Mr. Susabda is a graduate of Reformed Seminary, 1978, who has returned to his homeland to teach in a
Bible school there. He asked to be received by MTW in order to receive the prayer support of the PCA.
2. Mrs. Susabda lacks only one year before completing her work on an M.D. (medical doctor) degree. She
is currently working on that.
3. The family is supporting itself or being supported by friends and family in Indonesia.
4. If the school where Mr. Susabda is teaching signed an agreement with MTW (a foreign organization), it

<a id="ga08-p195"></a>
<!-- PAGE ga=8 pdf_page=195 printed_page=null -->

would seriously jeopardize its privilege of operation in a country that is extremely wary and defensive
toward outsiders intruding into the religious life of its people. (Missionaries are being expelled.)
5. MTW sent one of its own missionaries to see the administrator of the school and secured from him verbal
concurrence to all the terms of our cooperative agreement.
6. The administrator, not understanding why MTW was so insistent upon a signature (which he felt would
bring the government down upon his school), wondered if MTW was not trusting a Christian brother, and
why MTW was making so much to do about supporting a Christian brother, Jakub Susabda, with its
prayer.
7. M TW's only financial commitment to Jakub Susabda is to channel such funds as are specifically
designated for his support.

VID. WORLD RELIEF
This past Easter PCA families were being asked to bring one day’s pay as an offering for world hunger
and refugee relief. This report is being written before the results of that offering are in, but many PCA churches
were approaching this opportunity conscientiously and prayerfully. The final total of the offering for 1979,
channeled through World Relief, was $58,738.40.
IX. DAY OF PRAYER AND FASTING
The Day of Prayer for world evangelization on the third Sunday in May. gave PCA members an
opportunity to request prayer boxes from MTW. These boxes had cards with pictures, as well as biographical
and job information on all PCA missionaries. This project was very favorably received by those congregations
that heard and responded. Unfortunately, that did not prove to be large number of churches or individuals.
However, the program is still being offered, and the number of prayer partners is still growing.

XI. STRATEGY
It is the plan of the Committee on Mission to the World to maximize the PCA’s resources by bringing us
to the forefront of what God is doing in the world today. There are five areas that we consider strategic with
respect to what God is doing in the world:

<a id="ga08-p196"></a>
<!-- PAGE ga=8 pdf_page=196 printed_page=null -->

1. Unrcj. he.I Peoples
2. Thirii W,.iid Missions
3. Teams
4. "The whole counsel of God"
5. Student Involvement and Short-term Non-professional Missionaries.
To further clarify number 1, more and more today, reaching unreached peoples means reaching city
dwellers. It is estimated that by the year 2,000 86% of the world’s population will live in cities, and one in four
will live in a ol one million or more. Even as we plan on reaching blocks of people such as the Hindu people
and the Islamic people, we must consider cities as our prime target location.
Concerning number 5, we note that there has been a great deal of talk concerning anew student volunteer
movement in our generation. While we have been talking, God has been touching the hearts and calling out
young people to the point where there may be as many as 10,000-15,000 people involved in short-term
programs — many of them young people. This fact was further impressed on us at Urbana as one saw the
activities around booths such as Youth With a Mission, Operation Mobilization, Wycliffe Bible Translators
(which has a good short-term program), Cornelius Corps of Greater Europe Mission, and many others.
The point has come in missions where we should perhaps stop praying for another student volunteer
movement and start to create the structures that will enable us to incorporate large numbers of young people
whom the Loid has called and commissioned to do His work worldwide.
The present strategy of Mission to the World can be divided into two major and two minor areas. First, in
the area of church planting we can say that our primary purpose is to send and equip teams to plant churches in
responsive unreached urban centers. We further commit ourselves to the formation of presbyteries committed
to the whole counsel of God and participation with us as partners in world evangelization.
The second major area is the sending out of technical personnel under agencies who can insure proper
supervision and development of these personnel in order that they might in turn aid in the growth and
development of the church worldwide.
There are two minor areas which we should continue to emphasize. The first is the constant probing of
new areas where we can make a strategic impact with our church planting ministry. The second is sharing our
financial abundance with national brethren in projects that will have a crucial impact on world evangelization.
By God’s grace and with His continued enabling power, the Presbyterian Church in America through its
Committee on Mission to the World will continue to have an increasingly strategic part in fulfilling these goals
and others that He might place before us. To God be the glory. Great things He shall do.

#### XII. RECOMMENDATIONS

1. That the General Assembly express its gratitude for each of our missionaries and join in prayer for God's
provision of every spiritual, emotional and physical need in each of their lives.
2. That the General Assembly give praise to God for His provision of an increasing number of church-
planting candidates during 1979, and continue to pray that God will "send forth laborers into His
harvest."
3. That the General Assembly re-elect the Rev. Paul McKaughan as Coordinator and the Rev Kennedy
Smartt as Coordinator of Church Relations for the Committee on Mission to the World.
4. That the Assembly express its appreciation to Missionary-Evangelist Jimmy Lyons and the entire MTW
staff for their faithful and untiring service on behalf of our missionaries and Mission to the World.
5. That the third Sunday in May. 1981, be approved as a day of self-denial and prayer for world
evangelization in general and our own Mission to the World missionaries in particular.
6. That the budget of Mission to the World for 1981, as presented by the Stewardship Committee, be
approved.
7. That the changes to the MTW manual as presented to the Committee of Commissioners receive
provisional approval.
8. That the General Assembly authorize the Committee on Mission to the World to create a structure that
will enable students and those who are non-career missionaries to respond to God's call to short-term
work worldwide
